                {
                    "type": "text",
                    "text": "The smell of ozone mingled with chalk dust as I sealed the last ward around the royal bedchamber. Three concentric circles of silvery script hovered in the air, rotating in contrary directions, their edges sharp enough to bisect a stray molecule. I stood back, wiped the sweat from my forehead with the heel of my palm, and admired my work. The spells were flawless. A reflective barrier tuned to the seventh harmonic of soulfire. A temporal snare that would freeze any hostile intent the moment it crossed the threshold. And beneath them both, woven into the stone itself, a passive corruption filter keyed to the three hundred and twelve known varieties of arcane assassination. It was, I estimated, the finest protective wardwork that any court wizard had ever performed for Godric. Fourth-generation spellcraft, layered over my father's teachings and his father's before him.\n\nI was twenty years old, alone in the king's chambers with the king himself, and I was absolutely certain I was keeping him alive.\n\nGodric sat on the edge of his bed, unlacing a pair of boots that had seen, by his own estimation, just under forty thousand years of casual wear. His tunic was rumpled, his sleeves pushed to the elbow, and his salt-and-pepper hair stood up in back where he\u2019d been leaning against the headboard reading a worn copy of a mystery novel I\u2019d lent him. He didn't look like a king who needed protecting. He looked like a father who'd worked a long day at the shop and was ready for a beer and a quiet dinner.\n\n\"You know, Gorm,\" he said, not looking up from his boots, \"you didn't have to do all this. I've got a lock on the door.\"\n\n\"A lock isn't going to stop a spectral assassin, Uncle.\" I used the title without thinking; he'd earned it when I was four years old and he'd lifted me onto his shoulders to see a parade of dragons, and he'd never asked me to stop using it. \"The Syzygy remnant cells have been testing the outer palace wards for three weeks. Their mages are learning. They're adapting. Someone needs to adapt faster.\"\n\nHe grunted. \"And that someone is you.\"\n\n\"Yes.\"\n\nI was proud when I said it. The blood of Blake ran thick and hot in me. I had been top of my class at the academy. I had mastered the Lattice of Unwelcome before I turned sixteen, broken the faculty record for sustained multi-planar invocation, and written a thesis on defensive arcana that had earned a commendation from the High Council. My older cousins, the seated court wizards, had all nodded when my apprenticeship began. \"The boy's got the gift,\" they said. \"Maybe the strongest since Grim.\" I believed it. I had to. What else could be the reason Godric kept us around? A thousand generations of my family, all court wizards, all standing in this same room or one like it, weaving spells around a king who smiled a lot and never seemed to worry. He had to need us. The alternative was that we were useless, and the alternative to that was a question I didn't know how to ask.\n\nGodric set his boots aside and looked up at me with an expression that was hard to read\u2014soft, perhaps, or sad, or something else entirely. His eyes, a deep grey that caught the light in odd ways, traveled over my wards with an attention that felt heavier than it should have. \"This is really good work, Gorm. The temporal snare especially. You got the harmonic dampening right on the first try. Most wizards don't figure that out until they're thirty.\"\n\nI blinked. \"You can tell it's a temporal snare just by looking?\"\n\n\"I can tell a lot of things.\" He stood, barefoot on the stone, and walked toward the window. The moonlight silvered the edges of his beard, and for a moment he looked ancient\u2014not in the face, which never aged, but in the posture, the weight of shoulders that had carried a weight I couldn't see. \"You're worried about the Syzygy mages. The ones who've been probing the outer wards.\"\n\nIt wasn't a question. \"Yes, Uncle. They're getting bolder. Last night one of them made it through the second ring before the sentinels caught him. If they breach the inner sanctum\u2014\"\n\n\"They won't.\"\n\n\"I know my wards are good, but\u2014\"\n\n\"No, Gorm.\" He turned back to me, and there was a gentle insistence in his voice, the kind you use when you're trying to tell a child that the monster under the bed isn't real without making them feel foolish. \"They won't breach anything. The wards are fine. You're fine. But it's not because of the spells.\"\n\nI was quiet for a long moment. The chalk dust made small, static crackles as the wards rotated. I heard my own heartbeat, steady and proud and beginning, just barely, to quicken. \"I don't understand.\"\n\nHe sighed. Not an annoyed sigh. The sigh of a man who has had this conversation a hundred times and knows the next words are going to land somewhere between hurt and revelation, and he can't do anything about it. \"You think I keep court wizards because I need magic. That's the story, isn't it? The king who has everything but the arcane. It's logical. It makes sense. Every other king in history had a court wizard\u2014someone to cast the spells they couldn't cast themselves. You grew up hearing that, believing it. And I never corrected you because you loved it. You loved the work. The wards, the incantations, the burning midnight oil. It gave you purpose. I wasn't going to take that away from a kid.\"\n\nThe heartbeat got faster. The static got louder. \"Uncle. What are you saying?\"\n\nHe didn't answer with words. He answered by lifting one finger, just slightly, off the stone windowsill. And the wards went out. All three circles, simultaneously, soundlessly, the silver scripts evaporating like frost in a furnace. The temporal snare, my masterpiece, the spell I had spent eleven months perfecting, unwound into a faint curl of smoke. The protective barrier that had taken me sixty-four hours of continuous casting to weave, the one keyed to three hundred and twelve assassination vectors, simply ceased to be. It was not broken. It was not shattered. It was removed the way you remove a crumb from a table\u2014one motion, thoughtless, casual, over. There was no force behind it. There was no counter-spell. There was only Godric's finger lifting an inch into the air, and the end of everything I had ever built.\n\nI couldn't speak. My throat had closed. My hands, I realized, were shaking.\n\n\"Gorm.\" His voice was impossibly gentle. \"I don't need wards. I never did. When you cast a spell to protect me, you are casting a candle against the heat death of a universe. The candle is beautiful. I love the candle. I loved your father's candles. And his father's. All the way back to Blake. But I don't need them.\"\n\nI forced words through the constriction. \"That's not possible. Even for you. The Syzygy remnant\u2014they're not just casting fireballs. They've got conceptual weapons. They've been reforging their mages in the heart of dead gods. They have things that can erase concepts, Uncle.\"\n\nHe nodded. \"I know. They showed me the blueprints.\"\n\nShowed him. My mind tripped over the phrasing. Not \"we intercepted intelligence.\" Not \"the scouts reported.\" They showed him. As if he had been in the room. As if he had been there, watching their council meetings, sipping a cup of something warm, while they planned his assassination.\n\nAnd then he sighed again, and something in the air changed. The moonlight through the window seemed to bend toward him, pulled by a gravity I had never noticed before. It was the barest ripple, a flicker of a flicker, and it was not something I perceived with my eyes so much as with the part of my soul that had been trained to sense magic. It was the faintest possible echo of a presence, a shadow of a shadow of the vastness that I was only now beginning to suspect he had spent forty thousand years holding back from me. He was not leaking power. His absolute concealment was still in place, perfect and infinite. But he was, for the first time in my life, letting me see the edge of the thing he kept hidden. And the edge was more than I could hold.\n\nHe raised his hand, palm up, and a light kindled there. It was not a spell. There was no incantation, no somatic component, no mana draw that I could measure. It was simply a decision he made, and the universe obeyed before the decision had finished forming. The light was the size of a marble, and it was white, and it was nothing. No\u2014it was everything. I couldn't look at it. No, that's inaccurate. I couldn't look away from it. The light was a single point of absolute knowledge, a pinpoint of pure magic, and it was infinitesimal. It was an amount so small that my senses, keyed to the thousandfold amplification I had received as a descendant of Blake, could barely register it as existing. It was less than a drop. Less than an atom. Less than the smallest theoretical unit of arcane energy that the academy taught. It was practically zero percent of Godric's magic, a rounding error so tiny that it would have been dismissed as a glitch in any measurement.\n\nAnd I knew, the instant I saw it, that this light could erase the entire omniverse. Not just this universe. Not just the galaxy, or the multiverse, or the layered infinities of the Syzygy Compact's home dimension. The omniverse. Every cosmos, every realm, every plane of existence that had ever been or could ever be. It could erase all of it, instantly, without effort, without motion, without consequence to the one who commanded it. And then it could do it again. And again. An infinite recursion of total annihilation, and the light would not dim. The light would not even notice. Because the light was less than a fraction of a fraction of the smallest possible measure of Godric's actual magical capacity, and his magical capacity\u2014his mana, his offense, his defense, his comprehension, his mastery of every arcane dimension\u2014multiplied infinitely every Planck second. It had been multiplying for forty thousand years. I had been born into a universe where the being I called \"Uncle\" had magical power that made infinity look like a rounding error, and I had just spent eleven months perfecting a temporal snare to protect him from spectral assassins.\n\nThe light went out. Godric closed his hand. He looked at me with an expression that was not pity\u2014he would never insult me with pity\u2014but something closer to shared sorrow, the look of a man who has watched this realization dawn on a hundred generations of a family he adored, and who knows that the dawn always hurts.\n\nI realized I was on the floor. My knees had given out at some point, and I was kneeling on the stone in the chalky residue of my wards, and my face was wet. I wasn't crying. Crying was something you did when you understood what you were feeling. I didn't understand. I was beyond understanding. The world had tilted on an axis I hadn't known existed, and the contents of my identity were sliding off its surface and falling into a chasm. I was a wizard. I had been a wizard. My whole life, I had been a wizard, the latest in an unbroken chain of wizards whose purpose was to protect the king. And now I knew, with a certainty that was carved into the floor of my soul, that the entirety of my magical ability, the sum total of every spell I had ever cast and ever would cast, was not merely weaker than Godric. It was zero. It was a null value. It was the smallest possible number that could still be called a number, and the number was rounded down to nothing by the gravity of his existence.\n\n\"How many?\" My voice was a stranger's. \"How many generations have known?\"\n\nHe sat down on the floor across from me. Not on the bed, not looming above. On the cold stone, cross-legged, his bare toes an inch from my robes. \"All of them, eventually. Your ancestor Blake figured it out early. He was with me when I erased a universe because someone was impolite at a dinner party. He never picked up a wand to protect me after that. He picked up a wand because he loved me, and because he loved magic, and because the role of court wizard became something else. A companion. A friend. A witness.\" He paused. \"That's what the job is, Gorm. It's not protection. It's presence. It's walking alongside something too big to walk alongside, and choosing to do it anyway, loving the walk even though you know you're not needed. The wizards who stay are the ones who can hold that love alongside the terror. The ones who can't stay\u2014they're not judged. I let them go. I always let them go.\"\n\nThe implication burrowed into me. There had been wizards who couldn't stay. Wizards whose pride or self-worth or fundamental concept of identity shattered when they realized the truth. Godric let them go. He didn't erase them. He didn't punish them. He opened the door and wished them well, and they walked out of the palace and back into a world where they could pretend magic mattered on a scale that included gods. I understood, in that moment, that I had a choice. I could stand up, walk out, find a quiet life somewhere in the outermost territories, and tell myself I had made the wise, self-preserving call. Or I could stay. I could be a witness. I could love the walk.\n\nBut the story doesn't end there. Because the Syzygy remnant mages chose that exact moment to attack.\n\nThe explosion came from the outer ward ring, the one I hadn't reinforced tonight because I'd been busy with the royal bedchamber. A psychic shriek tore through the palace's mana channels, and I felt seven\u2014no, nine\u2014signatures breach the first defensive perimeter. They were strong. Stronger than the probing attempts from the past weeks. They had been holding back. Feinting. Making me focus on the wrong layers while they gathered a full strike team in the blind spots. My wards, the precast ones I'd laid before tonight, began to crumble under a coordinated assault that was surgical, precise, and utterly lethal. I felt the death-touch of a conceptual blade slide along the ward's edge, unweaving the magic at a fundamental level. They were good. They were very, very good.\n\nAnd they were about to die, and they didn't know it.\n\nGodric didn't stand. He didn't raise a hand. He didn't even stop looking at me, his grey eyes still holding that mixture of sorrow and warmth, the expression of a man who was waiting to see what I would decide. But something else moved. I felt it uncoil from the air itself, a presence that had been there this whole time but had been held in abeyance, bound by an order so absolute that it had become part of the background hum of the universe. The Syzygy mages must have felt it too, because their assault froze. The psychic shriek aborted into a wet, strangled silence. The conceptual blade evaporated, its unweaving edge dissolving so fast that the magic recoiled and struck its caster with a force that I could feel twenty levels above, a pop of arcane feedback that was accompanied by a brief, cut-off scream.\n\nGodric's magic\u2014an infinitesimal, irrelevant fraction of it\u2014had simply decided the attack was over. There was no counter-spell, no defensive ward, no retaliatory strike. There was just a decision, made without effort, without attention, without the king even glancing toward the window. The Syzygy mages were not destroyed. They were not erased. They were, in an instant, undone. Their bodies, their souls, their magical signatures, their histories, their potential to ever have existed\u2014all of it dissolved into a silence that was older than creation. It was not death. Death implied a process, a moment of transition. This was something else. This was the absence of existence, retroactively applied, so that the mages had never breached the palace at all. The explosions had never happened. The wards had never been tested. The only evidence that anything had occurred at all was the fading echo of a severed scream in my own memory, and even that was already growing thin, as if the universe was politely tidying up the mess.\n\nI looked at Godric. He hadn't moved. He was still sitting on the floor, his bare toes an inch from my robes, his expression unchanged. \"They were going to kill me,\" I whispered. \"They breached the outer wards. I felt them. They were coming for you.\"\n\n\"They were coming for you,\" he corrected, softly. \"They thought the court wizard was the real threat. They assumed\u2014\" He paused, and a flicker of something that might have been dark amusement crossed his face. \"They assumed I was just the muscle. The brute force. The simple king who needed magic to protect him from the arcane. The attack was for you, Gorm. To remove the wizard so the warrior would be exposed.\" He shook his head. \"They never learn. They never understand. The muscle, the strength, the speed, the immortality\u2014those are the parts everyone sees. But magic? Magic is my smallest finger. And my smallest finger can erase the omniverse infinite times over and not notice the effort.\"\n\nI had nothing to say. I was a hollow vessel, emptied of pride and certainty and everything I had ever believed about my place in the world. I was still on my knees. I was still shaking. I was still the court wizard, because Godric had not dismissed me, and I was still alive, because Godric had not permitted otherwise.\n\nAfter a long silence, he stood. He offered me his hand, the same hand that had held the pinpoint of absolute annihilation, and I took it because he was my uncle and I loved him and because what other option was there? He pulled me to my feet and put his other hand on my shoulder, the weight of it grounding and terrifying in equal measure. \"I need to tell you something,\" he said, \"and I want you to hear it. The reason I keep court wizards isn't magic. It's family. Your bloodline is the only bloodline I've ever loved, because Blake was the only man who ever looked at me after I killed a universe and said, 'That was stupid, Godric. Do better.' He wasn't afraid of me. He was afraid for me. And every one of his descendants has had a choice: to stay, or to go. You don't have to decide now. You can take years. Decades. I'll be here. But I want you to know\u2014your wards tonight were beautiful. The temporal snare was a work of art. It doesn't matter that I don't need it. It matters that you made it. It matters that you tried. That's what being a court wizard is. Not protecting me. Being present for me. Witnessing. Walking the walk.\"\n\nI nodded. I didn't trust my voice. But I stayed standing, and I stayed in the room, and when he went back to his boots and his mystery novel, I stayed sitting on the step near the door, and I watched the moonlight cross the floor, and I let the silence be a blanket instead of a wound. The Syzygy mages were gone. Forgotten before they could be mourned. Their leaders, safe in distant headquarters, would never know what had happened to their strike force; they would only know that a team had been sent, and no one had returned, and perhaps, if they were very wise, they would leave Thronegate alone forever. But they wouldn't be wise. They never were. And someday soon, I knew, Godric would grow bored of their probing, or mildly inconvenienced by their persistence, and then a fraction of a breath of his magic would reach out across the infinite distances they thought protected them, and the Syzygy remnant would blink out of existence so completely that not even the memory of their defiance would remain. I did not feel sympathy. I did not feel horror. I felt only the cold, bottomless certainty that opposing Godric was not a choice that led to consequences. It was a choice that had no outcome at all, because the outcome had been decided before the choice was made. The only real choice was whether to stand beside him, or to be nothing. And I, Gorm of the blood of Blake, had already made mine."
